Should match.\nThe TileLang kernel also computes in float32 and stores bf16.\nThe `dot` (raw_dot) should match exactly or very closely because the reduction order is deterministic.\nIn Triton, `tl.sum` does a deterministic tree reduction. On AMD, the hardware `v_add_f32` is deterministic.\nHowever, the order of accumulation of `x*k*w` across threads might differ from PyTorch (which uses vectorized operations in sorted order).\nPyTorch reference:\n`raw_dot = torch.einsum('...d,...d->...', x * wh, k_f * we)`\nHere `x * wh` is elementwise, then `einsum` does `sum((x*wh) * (k_f*we))`.\nThe accumulation order in PyTorch might be different from Triton's tree reduction.\nThis might cause slight differences in `raw_dot` beyond 2e-10?\nBut the test says TileLang passes with diff < 2e-10. TileLang uses warp_reduce_sum (tree reduction).\nTriton's `tl.sum` should produce very similar results.\nHowever, `raw_dot` is float32. Even accumulation order differences should be small for 4096 elements.\nLet's check: with random data in [-1, 1], sum of 4096 elements. Float32 has 24-bit mantissa. Worst-case cancellation error is ~4096 * epsilon * max_value ~ 4096 * 1e-7 * 1 = 4e-4. Relative error ~1e-7.\n`calc_diff` threshold is 2e-10.\nIf results differ by relative 1e-7, calc_diff would be ~1e-7 * 1e-7 = 1e-14? No.\n`calc_diff` measures the cosine distance: `1 - 2*<x,y> / (||x||^2 + ||y||^2)`.\nIf x and y differ elementwise by epsilon, then `<x,y> ≈ ||x||^2 - x*epsilon`. `||x||^2 + ||y||^2 ≈ 2||x||^2`.\nSo `calc_diff ≈ epsilon^2 / (2 ||x||^2) * N`. This is very small for small epsilon.\nFor `raw_dot` being a scalar, `calc_diff` is `1 - 2*x*y/(x^2+y^2)`.\nIf x and y differ by relative delta, `calc_diff ≈ delta^2 / 2`.\nFor delta ~ 1e-7, `calc_diff ≈ 5e-15`. Well below 2e-10.\nFor output bf16, the difference is bounded by bf16 quantization. `calc_diff` for random bf16 vectors should be tiny.\nSo correctness should be fine.\n\nOne more detail: `hidden_states` has shape `(num_tokens, hc_mult, hidden_size)`, `bfloat16`.\n`k` has shape `(num_tokens, hc_mult, hidden_size)`, `bfloat16`.\n`v` has shape `(num_tokens, hidden_size)`, `bfloat16`.\n`weight_fused` has shape `(hc_mult, hidden_size)`, `float32`.\n\nWait, `weight_fused` is `float32`. Good.\n\nLet's also check `v` stride. `v` is `(num_tokens, hidden_size)`. When we load `v` in the output pass, we load it for each head. So `v` is read redundantly.\nCan we launch 1 block per token, and have all `hc_mult` heads computed within the same block?\nYes! Instead of grid `(num_tokens * hc_mult,)`, we can use grid `(num_tokens,)` and each block computes all `hc_mult` heads.\nThis has advantages:\n- `v` is loaded only once per token (shared across heads).\n- `k` and `x` are contiguous across heads? `x` is `(T, H, D)`, so `head_id * stride(1)` is the offset.\n- We can loop over `hc_mult` heads within the block.\n\nHow many threads? If `num_tokens=8192`, grid=8192 blocks. Each block handles 4 heads.\nWith `num_warps=8` (512 threads) per block:\nFor each head, we process `hidden_size=4096` elements. 512 threads process 4096 elements in parallel.\nWe can do a loop over heads:\n```python\nfor head_id in range(hc_mult):\n    # load x_head, k_head, w_head\n    # compute reduction\n    # ...\n    # compute output for this head using v (loaded once)\n    # store output\n```\nWait, if we want to keep `x` in registers for the output, we need to process one head at a time.\nBut `v` can be loaded once and kept in registers/shared memory.\nLoading `v` once saves `3 * num_tokens * hidden_size * 2` bytes of read (since v is read 4 times normally, now 1 time).\nTotal read: x(256MB) + k(256MB) + v(64MB/4=16MB? No, still 64MB read once) + w(64KB).\nWait, if we read `v` once per block instead of 4 times:\nRead v = 8192 * 4096 * 2 = 64 MB (once per token).\nPreviously: 64MB * 4 = 256MB.\nSo we save 192MB of reads!\nTotal read: x(256) + k(256) + v(64) + w(~0) = 576MB.\nWrite: out(256) + intermediates(~0.5) = 256.5MB.\nTotal: 832.5MB.\nThis is SINGLE-PASS optimal! And we achieve it by processing multiple heads per block to share `v`.\n\nWait, can we keep `v` in registers while looping over heads?\n`v` is 4096 elements. For 512 threads, each thread holds 8 elements. That's 8 float32 = 32 bytes per thread. Total for wavefront: 8 VGPRs. Very cheap.\nSo in the block, we can load `v` once at the beginning:\n```python\nv = tl.load(v_base + offsets, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # keep in registers\nfor head_id in range(hc_mult):\n    x = tl.load(x_base + head_id * stride_x_h + offsets, ...).to(tl.float32)\n    k = tl.load(k_base + head_id * stride_k_h + offsets, ...).to(tl.float32)\n    w = tl.load(w_base + head_id * stride_w_h + offsets, ...).to(tl.float32)\n    acc_x_sq = tl.sum(x*x)\n    acc_k_sq = tl.sum(k*k)\n    acc_dot = tl.sum(x*k*w)\n    # compute gate\n    out = x + gate_val * v\n    tl.store(out_base + head_id * stride_out_h + offsets, out.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask)\n    if save_for_backward:\n        tl.store(dot_ptr + token_id * hc_mult + head_id, acc_dot)\n        ...\n```\nThis is beautiful! It processes all 4 heads in one block, shares `v` across them, and only loads `v` once.\nTotal threads per block: 512 (num_warps=8).\nGrid: `num_tokens` (8192 blocks).\nThis is a much better design.\n\nCan we keep `x` in registers for the output without reloading?\nIf we process one head at a time:\nLoad x, k, w -> compute sums -> compute gate -> compute out = x + gate * v -> store out.\n`x` is loaded once and used for both reduction and output. Since it's all in one code block (no loop over chunks), the compiler keeps `x` in registers.\nThis works because `BLOCK_SIZE = hidden_size` is handled in one `tl.load` call.\nWait, `x` is a block tensor of size `BLOCK_SIZE`. The compiler distributes it across threads. Each thread holds its slice. After `tl.sum`, the compiler should keep `x` alive because it's used in `out = x + gate * v`.\nYes! This is a true single-pass per head.\nAnd since we loop over `hc_mult` heads, `v` is loaded once and reused.\n\nTotal memory bandwidth: 832.5 MB.\nAt 4.0 TB/s, time = 208us. This is safely below 243us.\nAt 3.5 TB/s, time = 238us. Still below 243us!\nThis is great.\n\nBut what about the loop over `hc_mult`? `hc_mult=4` is small. The loop is unrolled by the compiler.\nThe `v` tensor is loaded once.\nFor each head, `x`, `k`, `w` are loaded.\nThis is exactly four single-passes, but `v` is read once and `x` is not reloaded (it's kept until after the store).\n\nWait, can the compiler keep `x` across the head loop if we reuse the variable `x`?\n```python\nfor head_id in range(hc_mult):\n    x = ...\n    ...\n    out = x + gate * v\n    store(out)\n```\nYes, `x` is scoped to the loop body. It's redefined each iteration. The compiler allocates registers per iteration. Since the iteration ends with the store, `x` is dead after that. No problem.\nAnd `v` is defined before the loop, so it's live across all iterations. Since `v` is 8 elements per thread and 4 iterations, the compiler should keep it in registers (8 VGPRs).\n\nWait, will the compiler be smart enough to keep `v` in registers if we unroll the loop? Yes.\n\nOne issue: `offsets = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)`. For `BLOCK_SIZE=4096`, this is a 1D block. With `num_warps=8`, each thread handles 8 elements.\n`v = tl.load(v_ptr + token_id * stride_v_t + offsets)`. This is a coalesced load.\nThen inside the head loop:\n`x = tl.load(x_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t + head_id * stride_x_h + offsets)`.\nSince `stride_x_h = hidden_size`, and `offsets` is contiguous, this is also coalesced.\n\nWhat about `num_tokens`? If `num_tokens=8192`, we have 8192 blocks. With 256 SMs, each SM gets 32 blocks.\nEach block handles 4 heads. Total wavefronts: 8192 blocks * 8 warps = 65536 wavefronts.\n256 CUs * 40 waves/CU = 10240 concurrent wavefronts. So there are ~6 waves of blocks to execute per CU. Excellent occupancy.\n\nThis design seems very strong.\n\nHowever, one concern: if `BLOCK_SIZE` = `hidden_size`, what about `hidden_size=7168`?\nWith `num_warps=8` (512 threads), each thread handles 14 elements.\nAs discussed, 14 is not a power of 2. Does this affect load vectorization?\nIf Triton generates loads of 4 elements (8 bytes) and 2 elements (4 bytes), it's fine.\nOr maybe it generates 8 + 4 + 2.\nThe performance impact might be negligible.\nAlternatively, for `hidden_size=7168`, we can use `num_warps=7` (448 threads) -> 16 elements/thread.\nOr `num_warps=14` (896 threads) -> 8 elements/thread.\nIs `num_warps=14` valid? It should be. `num_warps` is just the number of wavefronts per block.\nBut I don't know if Triton on AMD handles odd num_warps well. Probably it does, but it's safer to use powers of 2 for `num_warps`: 1, 2, 4, 8, 16.\nLet's check: 7168 / (8 * 64) = 14.\n7168 / (16 * 64) = 7. Not good.\n7168 / (4 * 64) = 28.\n7168 / (2 * 64) = 56.\n56 elements per thread is a lot, but the compiler can split into multiple load instructions.\nI think `num_warps=8` is fine for all.\n\nActually, for maximum safety and performance, I can choose `num_warps` dynamically:\n```python\ndef _choose_config(hidden_size):\n    # Try to pick num_warps such that hidden_size % (num_warps * 64) == 0 or close to it\n    # On AMD, wavefront size is 64.\n    warp_size = 64\n    best_nw = 8\n    # For 4096: 4096 / 512 = 8 (perfect)\n    # For 2048: 2048 / 512 = 4 (perfect)\n    # For 3072: 3072 / 512 = 6\n    # For 6144: 6144 / 512 = 12\n    # For 7168: 7168 / 512 = 14\n    # All are fine.\n    return 8\n```\n\nWhat about `BLOCK_SIZE`? Should it be `hidden_size` padded up to a multiple of 64 or 128?\nIf `hidden_size=576`, `BLOCK_SIZE=576`. 576 is a multiple of 64. Good.\nIf `hidden_size=2560`, 2560 is a multiple of 64. Good.\nIf `hidden_size=3072`, 3072 is a multiple of 64. Good.\nSo all hidden sizes from the generator are multiples of 64.\nWe can set `BLOCK_SIZE = hidden_size`.\n\nWait, `tl.load` with `BLOCK_SIZE=576` and `num_warps=8` (512 threads).\n`offsets = tl.arange(0, 576)`.\n`mask = offsets < 576`.\n512 threads load 576 elements.\nThreads 0..511: load elements 0..511.\nThreads 512+? There are only 512 threads. All 512 threads participate.\nBut `offsets` only has 576 elements. So most threads load 1 element. Or maybe threads 0..63 get a second element?\nActually, for a `tl.load` of shape (N,) across T threads, each thread might load `ceil(N/T)` elements.\nFor N=576, T=512: ceil(576/512)=2. With `BLOCK_SIZE=576`, it might use `block_shape=(576,)` and `num_elements_per_thread = BLOCK_SIZE // num_threads`.\nIf `BLOCK_SIZE=576` and `num_threads=512`, `576 // 512 = 1`. Element 512 exists. Threads 64..511 load 1 element.\nThis is exactly how Triton works for 1D blocks (cyclic distribution for SIMD efficiency).\nIn this case, `num_warps=8` means 512 threads. Threads 0..63 do extra work.\nVectorization: each thread loads 1 or 2 elements. 2 bf16 = 4 bytes. Probably still fine because the total memory volume is small.\nFor `hidden_size=4096`, `4096 // 512 = 8`. Each thread loads 8 elements = 16 bytes. Perfect 128-bit load.\n\nSo the main target for optimization is `hidden_size=4096` and `7168`.\nFor 4096, `num_warps=8` is perfect.\nFor 7168, `7168 // 512 = 14`. Very large vector, compiler splits into multiple instructions.\n`num_warps=2` for 7168: `7168 // 128 = 56`.\n`num_warps=1` for 7168: `7168 // 64 = 112`.\n\nSince we only need to beat TileLang by 2.5x and TileLang is at ~1.37TB/s, achieving 3.5-4TB/s with `num_warps=8` should be easy.\n\nLet me write and test the kernel.\n\nWait, one more potential issue: `.to(tl.float32)` for `x`, `k`, `v`, `w`.\nIf `x` is `tl.bfloat16`, `.to(tl.float32)` is a type conversion.\nOn AMD CDNA3, bf16 to fp32 conversion is native and fast.\nBut if we do `x * k * w`, it promotes to float32 automatically? Actually, if `x`, `k`, `w` are all float32, then yes.\nIn Triton, `tl.load` returns `tl.bfloat16`. So `v` is the same.\n\nIs there any issue with `k` and `v` strides in the actual model? The docstring doesn't say they are contiguous except the last dim. We should be robust to arbitrary strides on the first dimensions.\nBy computing offsets using strides, we are robust.\n`base_x = x_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t + head_id * stride_x_h`.\nThen `tl.load(base_x + offsets)`. This assumes `stride_x_d == 1` (last dim contiguous).\nIf the input is from `torch.randn`, the last dim is contiguous. The problem statement doesn't say.\nThe reference implementation doesn't make any stride assumptions but uses standard indexing.\nI'll assume `stride(-1) == 1` for all inputs. The test data is contiguous.\n\nLet me write the code.\n\n```python\nimport torch\nimport triton\nimport triton.language as tl\n\n@triton.jit\ndef _engram_gate_fwd_kernel(\n    x_ptr,\n    k_ptr,\n    v_ptr,\n    w_ptr,\n    out_ptr,\n    dot_ptr,\n    gate_ptr,\n    rstd_x_ptr,\n    rstd_k_ptr,\n    stride_x_t,\n    stride_x_h,\n    stride_k_t,\n    stride_k_h,\n    stride_v_t,\n    stride_w_h,\n    num_tokens,\n    hc_mult,\n    hidden_size,\n    eps,\n    clamp_value,\n    scalar,\n    save_for_backward: tl.constexpr,\n    BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr,\n):\n    pid = tl.program_id(0)\n    token_id = pid\n    if token_id >= num_tokens:\n        return\n    \n    x_base = x_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t\n    k_base = k_ptr + token_id * stride_k_t\n    v_base = v_ptr + token_id * stride_v_t\n    out_base = out_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t\n    \n    offsets = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)\n    mask = offsets < hidden_size\n    \n    # Load v once per token\n    v = tl.load(v_base + offsets, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n    \n    for head_id in range(hc_mult):\n        w_base_h = w_ptr + head_id * stride_w_h\n        \n        x = tl.load(x_base + head_id * stride_x_h + offsets, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n        k = tl.load(k_base + head_id * stride_k_h + offsets, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n        w = tl.load(w_base_h + offsets, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n        \n        acc_x_sq = tl.sum(x * x, axis=0)\n        acc_k_sq = tl.sum(k * k, axis=0)\n        acc_dot = tl.sum(x * k * w, axis=0)\n        \n        rstd_x_val = tl.rsqrt(acc_x_sq / hidden_size + eps)\n        rstd_k_val = tl.rsqrt(acc_k_sq / hidden_size + eps)\n        \n        dot_val = acc_dot * rstd_x_val * rstd_k_val * scalar\n        signed_sqrt = tl.where(dot_val >= 0, 1.0, -1.0) * tl.sqrt(tl.maximum(tl.abs(dot_val), clamp_value))\n        gate_val = tl.sigmoid(signed_sqrt)\n        \n        if save_for_backward:\n            out_idx = token_id * hc_mult + head_id\n            tl.store(dot_ptr + out_idx, acc_dot)\n            tl.store(gate_ptr + out_idx, gate_val)\n            tl.store(rstd_x_ptr + out_idx, rstd_x_val)\n            tl.store(rstd_k_ptr + out_idx, rstd_k_val)\n        \n        out = x + gate_val * v\n        tl.store(out_base + head_id * stride_x_h + offsets, out.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask)\n```\n\nWait, `stride_x_h` might not be `hidden_size` if the tensor is not standard contiguous. But for typical cases it is.\nSimilarly for `stride_k_h`.\nI should pass the actual strides.\n\nOne concern: `head_id * stride_x_h`. In Triton, `stride_x_h` is a scalar. Multiplying and adding is fine.\n\nWhat if `hc_mult` is not a power of 2? Here it's 4.\nThe loop `for head_id in range(hc_mult)` with `hc_mult=4` is unrolled by the Triton compiler (since `hc_mult` is passed as a runtime value, but small constant? So it's fine.\n\nNow, what about `num_warps`?\nI'll use `num_warps=8` for all hidden sizes.\nBut wait, for `hidden_size=576`, `BLOCK_SIZE=576`. `tl.arange(0, 576)`. Is this supported?\nYes, Triton supports any block size. But there might be a compiler bug or performance issue.\nTo be safe, we can pad `BLOCK_SIZE` to the next power of 2 or next multiple of 64.\nActually, padding to next power of 2:\n576 -> 1024.\n2048 -> 2048.\n2560 -> 4096.\n3072 -> 4096.\n4096 -> 4096.\n6144 -> 8192.\n7168 -> 8192.\nWith padded BLOCK_SIZE, more threads are masked, but `tl.arange(0, pow2)` might be easier for the compiler.\nHowever, it wastes memory bandwidth because the masked loads might still fetch cache lines.\nFor example, BLOCK_SIZE=4096 for hidden_size=3072: threads 0..3071 load valid data, threads 3072..4095 are masked. The 7th wavefront (threads 384..447) is fully active. The 8th wavefront (threads 448..511) is partially active (448..3071? No, 3072 > 512).\nWait, `offsets` is `tl.arange(0, 4096)`.\nThread i accesses elements `i, i+512, i+1024, ...` (cyclic).\nFor `hidden_size=3072`, elements 3072..4095 are out of bounds.\nThread i accesses `i + 5*512 = i + 2560`. If `i + 2560 < 3072`, i.e., `i < 512`, valid.\nThread i accesses `i + 6*512 = i + 3072`. If `i + 3072 < 3072`, i.e., `i < 0`, invalid. So all 512 threads are fully active for the first 6 elements.\nWait, this is the cyclic distribution. Let's verify.\nFor a block of shape (N,) with N=4096 and num_threads=512:\nThread 0 gets indices 0, 512, 1024, 1536, 2048, 2560, 3072, 3584.\nFor N=3072, thread 0 accesses 0..2560 (valid), 3072 (invalid), 3584 (invalid).\nSo mask[3072]=False, mask[3584]=False for thread 0.\nThread 0 does 6 valid accesses + 2 masked accesses.\nThread 511 accesses 511, 1023, 1535, 2047, 2559, 3071, 3583, 4095.\nFor N=3072, 511..3071 are valid. 3583 and 4095 are invalid.\nSo EVERY thread has 6 valid and 2 masked accesses.\nThis means exactly 25% of the loads are masked and potentially wasteful!\nThe hardware might still issue the loads and then discard results. That wastes 25% of memory bandwidth.\nThis is BAD.\n\nSo padding `BLOCK_SIZE` to a power of 2 when `hidden_size` is not a power of 2 is a bad idea!\nIt's much better to set `BLOCK_SIZE = hidden_size` exactly.\nFor `hidden_size=3072`, `BLOCK_SIZE=3072`.\nThread 0 accesses 0, 512, 1024, 1536, 2048, 2560. When it's False, the stores to dot/gate/rstd are removed by the compiler.\nWhen False, the caller still passes `dot_ptr` etc. but they are None. The kernel won't use it because `save_for_backward` is False, so the stores are compiled out. But Triton still expects a valid pointer argument. Passing `hidden_states` is fine since stores are elided.\nHowever, a cleaner way is to pass a small dummy tensor or just `hidden_states` (since it will not be written to). But `tl.constexpr` is better for optimization.\n\nOne more thing: strides of `hidden_states`, `k`, `v`.\nI should pass `hidden_states.stride(0), hidden_states.stride(1)` etc.\nAnd use them in the kernel: `tl.load(x_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t + head_id * stride_x_h + offsets)`.\nThis is correct.\n\nWait, what if `hidden_states.stride(2) != 1`? The problem likely assumes it is, but to be safe, we can assert `hidden_states.stride(-1) == 1` and `k.stride(-1) == 1` and `v.stride(-1) == 1` like TileLang does. Should be contiguous.\n\nI think the kernel is ready. Let's write it to `/app/engram_triton.py` and benchmark.\n\nWait, should I consider using `tl.cdiv`? No, not needed.\n\nOne more thought: Triton on AMD sometimes has issues with loads inside loops that are not unrolled. Our `for head_id in range(hc_mult)` is a dynamic loop. `hc_mult=4`. Triton should compile it as a loop.\nBut is there any performance degradation from the loop? No, 4 iterations is fine.\nHowever, `v` stays in registers across the loop. The compiler must not spill it.\n`v` is a block tensor of `BLOCK_SIZE` elements.\nWait, `v` is defined outside the loop as `v = tl.load(...)`. It is a block tensor distributed across threads.\nIf `BLOCK_SIZE=4096` and `num_threads=512`, each thread holds 8 float32 values of `v`. Total 8 VGPRs.\nAcross 4 iterations, these 8 VGPRs must stay allocated. We compute it in Python before launching the kernel and pass it as `BLOCK_SIZE=block_size` and `NUM_ITERS=num_iters`. Since these are Python ints, triton compiles the kernel with these values as constexprs.\n\nHowever, Triton maintains a cache of compiled kernels keyed by these constexpr values. Unlikely because of the dependency on the reduction.\n\nGiven the huge speedup margin, we can just use the double-load loop. It will be simpler and still well within the 2.5x target.\n\nBut let's also think about using `tl.reduce` or `tl.sum` across a block. In the current kernel, we do `tl.sum(x * x, axis=0)` which reduces across the block dimension. For a block of 4096 and 256 threads, Triton will generate efficient warp shuffles. For smaller blocks (e.g., 1024 with 256 threads), each thread handles 4 elements; tl.sum across the block still uses warp reductions.\n\nOne more issue: `range(NUM_ITERS)` where `NUM_ITERS` is a constexpr. This is fine. For `dot_val` near zero, it returns 0.0, and gate becomes `sigmoid(0.0) = 0.5`. The reference uses `dot.sign()` which in PyTorch returns 0 for 0. However, the reference then computes `signed_sqrt = dot.abs().clamp_min(clamp_value).sqrt() * dot.sign()`. For dot=0, `signed_sqrt = sqrt(clamp_value) * 0 = 0`. So gate = sigmoid(0) = 0.5. So using `sign` that returns 0 for 0 is important for the exact edge case, but our `where` does that.\n\nWait, `dot.sign()` returns 0 for 0, but `clamp_min` ensures `abs(dot) >= clamp_value`. So if dot=0, `abs(0).clamp_min(1e-6) = 1e-6`, sqrt = 0.001, times sign(0) = 0. So exactly 0. Our code: `tl.where(dot_val > 0.0, 1.0, tl.where(dot_val < 0.0, -1.0, 0.0))` gives 0 if dot==0. `tl.sqrt(tl.maximum(tl.abs(dot_val), clamp_value))` gives sqrt(clamp_value). signed_sqrt = 0 * sqrt(clamp_value) = 0. Good.\n\nPrecision: the loaded x, k, w are converted to float32 before multiplication. The reference does everything in float32. Our code does that. The TileLang kernel also does that.\n\nOne subtle difference: order of operations in accumulating dot. We accumulate `x*k*w` in float32. The reference uses `torch.einsum('...d,...d->...', x * wh, k_f * we)`. This computes elementwise product `x*wh` then `k_f*we` then dot product. The order is `(x * w) * k`. Ours is `x * k * w`. In floating point, multiplication is not strictly associative, so the order might produce tiny differences. To match the reference exactly, we might need to compute `x * w` first, then multiply by `k`. Or `x * (k * w)`. The TileLang kernel does: `gate_score_local[0] += x_local[i_k] * w_local[i_k] * k_local[i_k]`. So it does `x * w * k`. The multiplication of 4 numbers is usually done sequentially: first `x * wh`, then `* k_f`, then `* we`, or some optimized path. The order is not guaranteed across all implementations. However, for our correctness check, we need `calc_diff < 2e-10`. The difference due to associativity is usually tiny.\n\nBut the TileLang kernel does `x * w * k`, i.e., `((x * w) * k)`. Not needed.\n\nI will now rewrite the kernel and test.\n\nLet's draft the code:\n\n```python\n@triton.jit\ndef _engram_gate_fwd_kernel(\n    x_ptr,\n    k_ptr,\n    v_ptr,\n    w_ptr,\n    out_ptr,\n    dot_ptr,\n    gate_ptr,\n    rstd_x_ptr,\n    rstd_k_ptr,\n    stride_x_t,\n    stride_x_h,\n    stride_k_t,\n    stride_k_h,\n    stride_v_t,\n    stride_w_h,\n    num_tokens,\n    hc_mult,\n    hidden_size,\n    eps,\n    clamp_value,\n    scalar,\n    save_for_backward: tl.constexpr,\n    BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr,\n    NUM_ITERS: tl.constexpr,\n):\n    pid = tl.program_id(0)\n    token_id = pid\n    if token_id >= num_tokens:\n        return\n\n    x_base = x_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t\n    k_base = k_ptr + token_id * stride_k_t\n    v_base = v_ptr + token_id * stride_v_t\n    out_base = out_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t\n\n    offsets = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)\n\n    acc_x_sq = 0.0\n    acc_k_sq = 0.0\n    acc_dot = 0.0\n\n    for i in range(NUM_ITERS):\n        idx = i * BLOCK_SIZE + offsets\n        mask = idx < hidden_size\n        x = tl.load(x_base + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n        k = tl.load(k_base + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n        # Wait, we need to include head_id in the offset.\n```\n\nOh! Let's see.\n\nLet's code this up.\n\nOne more thing: In the original reference, the `dot` is computed as `raw_dot = torch.einsum('...d,...d->...', x * wh, k_f * we)`. The `x * wh` is computed element-wise first, then `k_f * we`, then dot. In the TileLang kernel, the accumulation is `x_local * w_local * k_local`. Our fast kernel also uses `x * k * w`. For maximum consistency with the reference, we could compute `tmp = x * w; dot = tl.sum(tmp * k)`. But for the diff we saw, it's okay.\n\nHowever, to ensure the loop kernel passes the strictest threshold, let's compute it as `tl.sum((x * w) * k)` (matching reference's left-to-right after grouping). Actually, mathematically it's the same, but the floating point rounding might differ. Let's test both.\n\nActually, the PyTorch reference is `torch.einsum('...d,...d->...', x * wh, k_f * we)`. The `einsum` likely computes `(x * wh) * (k_f * we)` and then sums. The TileLang computes `x * w * k`. Our code computes `x * k * w`. Since multiplication is commutative in real numbers but not in floats, the order matters slightly. Let's align with the reference: compute `x * w` first, then multiply by `k`, then sum. So `tl.sum((x * w) * k)`. Let's change the fast kernel too.\n\nWait, for the fast kernel we already tested, changing the order might change the diff vs reference but shouldn't affect vs TileLang significantly. Let's test.\n\nAlso, we need to ensure that `hidden_size` is cast to float for division `acc_x_sq / hidden_size`. We use `hidden_size` which is an int; Triton will promote to float in `tl.rsqrt(acc_x_sq / hidden_size + eps)`. That's fine.\n\nLet's draft the final code.\n\n```python\n\"\"\"Implement the Engram gate forward pass as a Triton kernel.\"\"\"\nimport torch\nimport triton\nimport triton.language as tl\n\n\n@triton.jit\ndef _engram_gate_fwd_kernel_fast(\n    x_ptr,\n    k_ptr,\n    v_ptr,\n    w_ptr,\n    out_ptr,\n    dot_ptr,\n    gate_ptr,\n    rstd_x_ptr,\n    rstd_k_ptr,\n    stride_x_t,\n k_base = k_ptr + token_id * stride_k_t\n    v_base = v_ptr + token_id * stride_v_t\n    out_base = out_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t\n\n    offsets = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)\n    mask = offsets <      out = x + gate_val * v\n            tl.store(out_base + head_id * stride_x_h + idx, out.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask)\n\n\ndef engram_gate_fwd_triton(\n    hidden_states: torch.Tensor,\n    k:          output,\n            _dot_ptr,\n            _gate_ptr,\n            _rstd_x_ptr,\n            _rstd_k_ptr,\n            hidden_states.stride(0),\n            hidden_states.stride(1),\n            k.stride(0),\n            k.stride(1),\n            v.stride(0),\n            weight_fused.stride(0),\n            num_tokens,\n            hc_mult,\n            hidden_size,\n            eps,\n           num_warps=8,\n        )\n\n    return output, dot, gate_score, rstd_x, rstd_k\n```\n\nLet's test this code.\n\nOne concern: `hidden_size & (hidden_size - 1) == 0` is true for powers of two, tl.where(dot_val > 0.0, 1.0, tl.where(dot_val < 0.0, -1.0, 0.0))\n        signed_sqrt = sign_val * tl.sqrt(tl.maximum(tl.abs(dot_val), clamp_value))\n        gate_val = tl.sigmoid(signed_sqrt)\n\n          out_idx = token_id * hc_mult + head_id\n            tl.store(dot_ptr + out_idx, acc_dot)\n            tl.store(gate_ptr + out_idx, gate_val)\n            tl.store(rstd_x_ptr + out_idx, rstd_x_val)\n            tl.store(rstd_k_ptr + out_idx, rstd_k_val)\n\n        out = x + gate_val * v\n        tl.store(out_base + head_id * stride_x_h + offsets, out.to(tl.bfloat16), save_for_backward: tl.constexpr,\n    BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr,\n    NUM_ITERS: tl.constexpr,\n):\n    \"\"\"Multi-tile fallback for very large hidden sizes.\"\"\"\n    token_id = tl.program_id(0)\n out_ptr + token_id * stride_x_t\n\n    offsets = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)\n\n    for head_id in range(hc_mult):\n        w_base_h = w_ptr + head_id * stride_w_h\n\n        acc_x_sq = 0.0\n        acc_k_sq = 0.0\n        acc_dot = 0.0\n        for i in range(NUM_ITERS):\n            idx = i * BLOCK_SIZE + offsets\n            mask = idx < hidden_size\n            x = tl.load(x_base + head_id * stride_x_h + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n            k = tl.load(k_base + head_id * stride_k_h + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n            w = tl.load(w_base_h + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n            acc_x_sq += tl.sum(x * x, axis=0)\n            acc_k_sq += tl.sum(k * k, axis=0)\n            acc_dot += tl.sum((x * w) * k, axis=0)\n\n        rstd_x_val = tl.rsqrt(acc_x_sq / hidden_size + eps)\n        rstd_k_val = tl.rsqrt(acc_k_sq / hidden_size + eps)\n\n        dot_val = acc_dot * rstd_x_val * rstd_k_val * scalar\n        sign_val = tl.where(dot_val > 0.0, 1.0, tl.where(dot_val < 0.0, -1.0, 0.0))\n        signed_sqrt = sign_val * tl.sqrt(tl.maximum(tl.abs(dot_val), clamp_value))\n        gate_val = tl.sigmoid(signed_sqrt)\n\n tl.store(rstd_x_ptr + out_idx, rstd_x_val)\n            tl.store(rstd_k_ptr + out_idx, rstd_k_val)\n\n        for i in range(NUM_ITERS):\n            idx = i * BLOCK_SIZE + offsets\n            mask = idx < hidden_size\n            x = tl.load(x_base + head_id * stride_x_h + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n            v = tl.load(v_base + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)\n            out = x + gate_val * v\n            tl.store(out_base + head_id * stride_x_h + idx, out.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask)\n\n\ndef engram_gate_fwd_triton(\n    hidden_states: torch.Tensor,\n    k: torch.Tensor,\n    v: torch.Tensor,\n    weight_fused: torch.Tensor,\n    eps: float,\n    clamp_value: float,\n    save_for_backward: bool = True,\n):\n    num_tokens, hc_mult, hidden_size = hidden_states.shape\n    scalar = hidden_size ** -0.5\n\n    output = torch.empty_like(hidden_states)\n    if save_for_backward:\n        dot = torch.empty((num_tokens, hc_mult), dtype=torch.float32, device=hidden_states.device)\n        gate_score = torch.empty((num_tokens, hc_mult), dtype=torch.float32, device=hidden_states.device)\n        rstd_x = torch.empty((num_tokens, hc_mult), dtype=torch.float32, device=hidden_states.device)\n        rstd_k = torch.empty((num_tokens, hc_mult), dtype=torch.float32, device=hidden_states.device)\n    else:\n        dot = gate_score = rstd_x = rstd_k = None\n\n    grid = (num_tokens,)\n\n    _dot_ptr = dot if save_for_backward else hidden_states\n    _gate_ptr = gate_score if save_for_backward else hidden_states\n    _rstd_x_ptr = rstd_x if save_for_backward else hidden_states\n    _rstd_k_ptr = rstd_k if save_for_backward else hidden_states\n\n    # Pick a single power-of-two block that covers the hidden dimension.\n    # This avoids loop-accumulation rounding differences vs the reference.\n    block_size = _next_power_of_2(hidden_size)\n\n    # Cap block_size to avoid excessive register pressure (8192 is plenty).\n    MAX_BLOCK = 8192\n    if block_size <= MAX_BLOCK:\n        _engram_gate_fwd_kernel_flat[grid](\n            hidden_states,\n            k,\n            v,\n            weight_fused,\n            output,\n    k.stride(1),\n            v.stride(0),\n            weight_fused.stride(0),\n            num_tokens,\n            hc_mult,\n            hidden_size,\n            eps,\n            clamp_value,\n            scalar,\n            save_for_backward=save_for_backward,\n            BLOCK_SIZE=block_size,\n            num_warps=8,\n        )\n    else:\n        tile = hidden_size & -hidden_size  # largest power-of-two divisor\n        num_iters = hidden_size // tile\n        _engram_gate_fwd_kernel_loop[grid](\n            hidden_states,\n            k,\n            v,\n            weight_fused,\n            output,\n            _dot_ptr,\n            _gate_ptr,\n            _rstd_x_ptr,\n            _rstd_k_ptr,\n            hidden_states.stride(0),\n            hidden_states.stride(1),\n            k.stride(0),\n            k.stride(1),\n            v.stride(0),\n            weight_fused.stride(0),\n            num_tokens,\n            hc_mult,\n            hidden_size,\n            eps,\n            clamp_value,\n            scalar,\n            save_for_backward=save_for_backward,\n            BLOCK_SIZE=tile,\n            NUM_ITERS=num_iters,\n
